// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Hebrew Bible (Yahweh-Leviathan and Yahweh-Rahab, Ch. 8) — protective-versus-adversarial dragon contrast: the Hebrew-Bible Leviathan and Rahab corpus presents the dragon as adversary of Yahweh's cosmic order, with the slaying / binding of the dragon being the cosmic-order-establishing act in the Psalter / Isaiah / Job / Habakkuk attestations; the Bida narrative presents the serpent as *protector* of the city's order, with the killing being the order-destroying act — the inversion of the protective-or-adversarial role of the serpentine figure being the load-bearing structural feature that distinguishes the Bida case from the Hebrew-Bible material. The Hebrew-Bible-versus-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the level of the *role of the serpent* (protective rather than adversarial) within the order-establishment / order-maintenance system.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Egypt (Ra-Apep, Ch. 16) — cyclical-maintenance-register contrast: the daily Apep-overthrowing ritual of the Egyptian solar-cycle is the *cyclical-maintenance* practice in the strong sense, in which the Eurasian combat-and-overthrowing of the chaos-bringer is *re-enacted* daily as the means of maintaining the cosmic order; the annual maiden-tribute to Bida is structurally a *cyclical-maintenance* practice in the protective-serpent register, in which the human-and-divine cycle of ritual observance maintains the city's prosperity *with* the protector-serpent rather than against an adversary — and the killing of Bida *breaks* the maintenance cycle rather than re-enacts it. The Apep-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the cyclical-maintenance level (per scope.md §"Subject definition" combat-myth register 3) — the most precise structural register at which the inversion operates.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Anatolia (Illuyanka, Ch. 6) — *purulli*-festival cyclical-maintenance contrast: the Hittite Illuyanka material's *purulli*-festival ritual context is the cyclical-maintenance register in the explicit-festival-attached form, with the Tarḫunna-versus-Illuyanka combat-narrative recited as the festival's textual centre and the festival itself maintaining the cosmic-and-political order across the annual cycle; the Bida narrative's annual maiden-tribute is structurally a counterpart cyclical-maintenance practice but in the protective-serpent register rather than the adversary-serpent register, and the killing of Bida breaks the maintenance cycle rather than re-enacting it. The Illuyanka-Bida contrast is registered for Part II C7 as a worked instance of the inversion at the cyclical-maintenance / festival-attached level.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Americas (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — order-establishment contrast at the paradigmatic non-Eurasian level: Huitzilopochtli's slaying of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua at Coatepec is read by the Mexica-area-specialist tradition (Matos Moctezuma, Carrasco, López Austin) as a paradigmatic establishment-of-cosmic-order-and-sovereignty combat — the Mexica-state-political-ritual-centre version of the chaoskampf order-establishment pattern at full strength; the Bida combat is the inversion case at the same non-Eurasian paradigmatic level — the slaying-ENDS-prosperity inversion held against the slaying-ESTABLISHES-sovereignty paradigm. The Coatepec-Bida contrast within the present book's non-Eurasian-cluster chapters is registered for Part II C7 as the principal worked instance of the *inversion within the non-Eurasian cluster* methodological move.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Pariacaca and the amaru (Ch. 26) — co-related-tier non-Eurasian chapter where the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing on grounds of an alternative load-bearing function (wak'a-succession in the Pariacaca case; covenant-and-consequence in the Bida case); the Pariacaca cycle is the *transformation* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category transforms when area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing) and the Bida narrative is the *inversion* sub-problem case (chaoskampf category's structural features present with valences reversed); the two chapters between them anchor the transformation and inversion sub-problems of Part II C7 from different non-Eurasian-cluster locations within the present book — Pariacaca from the Andean cluster and Bida from the West African cluster. The Pariacaca-Bida pairing is the present book's principal worked instance of the structural distinction between transformation and inversion within Part II C7.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mapuche Kai-Kai vs. Tren-Tren (Ch. 27) — non-Eurasian-cluster co-comparandum: the Mapuche material is the South-Cone non-Eurasian comparandum to the present chapter's West-African non-Eurasian comparandum, with the two chapters between them representing the principal non-Eurasian-cluster cases beyond the Americas and Africa primary anchors of chapters 24 and 26; the Mapuche material is registered here as a forward hook for Part II C7 without consultation of the Mapuche-chapter material itself per the parallel-agent task-discipline requirement.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mwindo and Kirimu (Ch. 29) — Africa-cluster contrastive case: where Mwindo's slaying of Kirimu is followed by the hero's punishment by the sky-god Nkuba (per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml on the contrastive-case classification), the Bida narrative is followed by the abandonment of the polity rather than by the hero's punishment — the two Africa-cluster chapters between them representing the *post-killing punishment* (Mwindo) and *post-killing dispersal* (Bida) variants of the slaying-the-protector-rather-than-establishing-order narrative pattern, with the Mwindo case being the contrastive-case-tier classification and the Bida case being the related-tier inversion-anchor classification. The Mwindo-Bida pairing within the Africa cluster is registered for Part II C7 as the principal worked instance of the *Africa-cluster post-killing-consequence* methodological move.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the present chapter is a worked instance of three methodological moves: (1) the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement (scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3) applied to oral-tradition material, with the chapter opening with the Soninke tradition's covenant-and-consequence framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category; (2) the inversion-frame methodological move (the use of the comparativist inversion-frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation), which the present chapter establishes for the present book and which Part II C7 (Ch. 40) develops at full length; (3) the named-but-flagged secondary-specialists pattern for non-Eurasian non-textual traditions (Frobenius, Monteil, Dieterlen, Hale, Bird, Levtzion named in body prose alongside the Conrad and Belcher anchors but not load-bearing for any individual EVIDENCE-anchored claim), per the Ch23 FC-02 and Ch26 FC-02 named-but-flagged precedent.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, Ch. 40) — PRIMARY FORWARD TARGET. The present chapter is the PRINCIPAL LOAD-BEARING TEST CASE for the *inversion* sub-problem proper (chaoskampf category's structural features present with valences reversed at every load-bearing point), to be paired in C7 with the *transformation* sub-problem cases of chapter 23 (Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi as sword-etiology / metallurgical-allegory) and chapter 26 (Pariacaca cycle as wak'a-succession). The chapter's load-bearing methodological move — using the inversion frame as a heuristic for cross-cultural reflection rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation — is the move that C7 is to investigate at full length, and the chapter's [INFERENCE] marker on the inversion reading and [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker on the Mande-area-specialist objection are the principal markers that Part II C7 is to engage with directly. Per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the PRIMARY ANCHOR CASE classification.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem, Ch. 41) — the present chapter contributes the *area-specialist objection to typological-assimilation* sub-problem to C8 alongside the Susanoo / Orochi material (Ch. 23), the Pariacaca cycle (Ch. 26), the Mwindo / Kirimu material (Ch. 29), the Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material (Ch. 32), and the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks); the Mande-area-specialist objection is to typological-assimilation rather than to combat-myth-recognition as such, and is on the spectrum of objection-strengths between the Naumann objection in Ch23 (combat-myth framing as such) and the Salomon-Urioste-Taylor-Dean objection in Ch26 (chaos-order framing but not combat-myth framing as such); the present chapter's appropriation-protocol-compliant treatment is to use the inversion frame as the heuristic that registers the area-specialist objection rather than as a typology that imposes the Eurasian pattern on the Mande-area material.

# Input file: chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.adoc

[[ch-29-mwindo-kirimu]]
== Mwindo and Kirimu (the Contrastive Case)

=== Source overview

The story this chapter follows is a Nyanga oral epic in which the hero Mwindo, after a sequence of feats and journeys, kills the dragon Kirimu in the forest and is then struck down by the sky-god Nkuba and taken up to the celestial realm where he is humiliated, instructed in the limits of his power, and returned to the human world chastened. The killing of the dragon is not the establishment of cosmic order or sovereignty but a transgression that the sky-god punishes; the moral-didactic point of the episode is the hero's maturation through humiliation rather than the defeat of the dragon as such.footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml summary on Mwindo's killing of Kirimu, on Nkuba's subsequent punishment of Mwindo for the killing, on the inverted moral valence of the dragon-slaying as hubris rather than order-making, and on the hero's required humiliation and re-integration as the narrative's didactic centre; Biebuyck, D. and Mateene, K.C. (trans. and eds.), *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)* (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1969; ISBN 9780520003767), Kirimu episode and following celestial-journey passages, on the killing of the dragon, the sky-god's lightning-strike, and the celestial humiliation of the hero.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The first thing to register about that narrative is the shape of the evidence behind it. The story survives in oral-tradition form among the Nyanga (Banyanga) people of the upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested terrain in the Kivu region of what was at the time of recording the eastern Belgian Congo (now eastern Democratic Republic of the Congo), with no pre-modern written attestation in any African or external manuscript tradition. The textual witness situation for the present chapter is therefore the body of mid-twentieth-century ethnographic recordings of Nyanga oral-narrative performances by named tradition-bearers, supplemented by the comparative African-oral-epic critical-anthology scholarship of the later twentieth century.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the Nyanga (Banyanga) people of the upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested Kivu-region terrain in the eastern Belgian Congo as the tradition-bearing community for the Mwindo material, on the oral-tradition character of the narrative, and on the absence of any pre-modern written attestation in African or external manuscript form; Belcher, S., *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University Press, 1999; ISBN 9780253212818), ch. on Mwindo, on the Nyanga tradition's place within the wider African oral-epic comparative apparatus and on the mid-twentieth-century ethnographic-recording corpus as the principal textual witness situation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's primary anchor is Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene's *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*, published by the University of California Press in 1969. It is the standard English-language critical edition and translation of the epic, drawn from a single recorded performance — recorded in 1956 at Bese, a Nyanga locality on the upper Oso river in the Kivu region, from the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke (conventionally cited in the scholarship as Shé-Kárisi Rureke). Mateene himself was a Nyanga-speaking collaborator and Biebuyck the principal field-ethnographer.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the recording-history of the present text — recorded in 1956 at Bese on the upper Oso river in the Kivu region from the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke (conventionally cited as Shé-Kárisi Rureke); on Kahombo C. Mateene as Nyanga-speaking collaborator and Daniel Biebuyck as principal field-ethnographer; on the *Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga* as the standard English-language critical-edition-and-translation anchor of the epic.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The performance from which the Biebuyck-Mateene 1969 text derives was extended in time and structured by the conventions of Nyanga narrative-and-song performance. The narrator delivered the epic in successive sessions over a period of several days, with the narrative structured by alternating spoken passages, sung passages, formulaic refrains, and the narrator's interaction with a small ensemble of accompanists; the recorded text is presented in the 1969 edition with explicit notation of the spoken-and-sung alternation and the formulaic-refrain structure of the performance.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the extended-time and conventionalised-structure of the 1956 Bese performance from which the present text derives, on the alternation of spoken and sung passages, on the formulaic refrains, on the narrator's interaction with a small ensemble of accompanists, and on the 1969 edition's explicit notation of the spoken-and-sung alternation and formulaic-refrain structure of the performance.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The 1956 Rureke performance is one of several Nyanga epic-performances Biebuyck recorded in the Kivu region during his fieldwork of the 1950s. Biebuyck and successors subsequently published further Nyanga epic-material drawn from other named narrators — but the 1969 Rureke text is the standard primary text for the present chapter, with the further published Nyanga epic-material treated as related comparative material rather than as parallel attestation of the Mwindo-Kirimu episode.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the 1956 Rureke performance as one of several Nyanga epic-performances Biebuyck recorded in the Kivu region during his 1950s fieldwork, and on Biebuyck and successors subsequently publishing further Nyanga epic-material from other named narrators (notably Biebuyck's later *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga*, University of California Press, 1978, named here in body prose only — the 1978 volume is not currently in sources.yaml and is cited indirectly through Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999); per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml variants.classification on the single-prevalent treatment with Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 (Rureke text) as the chapter's anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's secondary scholarly anchor for the wider African oral-epic comparative context is Stephen Belcher's *Epic Traditions of Africa*, published by Indiana University Press in 1999. Belcher's chapter on the Mwindo epic situates the Nyanga material within the comparative apparatus of African oral-epic scholarship — alongside the Mande Sunjata corpus, the Bambara Bamana hunters' epic tradition, the Wolof Kajoor cycle, the Fang *mvet* tradition, and the Bantu epic corpus more broadly — and supplies the comparative-scholarly framing within which the Nyanga material is read as a flagship case of the central-African forest-zone oral epic rather than within the chaoskampf-typology register imported from Eurasian combat-myth scholarship.footnote:[Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo, on the comparative situation of the Nyanga Mwindo material within the wider African oral-epic apparatus alongside the Mande Sunjata corpus, the Bambara hunters' epic tradition, the Wolof Kajoor cycle, the Fang *mvet* tradition, and the wider Bantu epic corpus; on the Mwindo epic as a flagship case of the central-African forest-zone oral epic; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml sources.primary entry on Belcher 1999 as principal secondary anchor for the comparative oral-epic context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

A further scholarly anchor sits behind the wider comparative-tradition framing rather than the source-content. Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, supplies the long-arc combat-myth typology against which the Mwindo / Kirimu narrative is read as the present book's clearest contrastive-case-tier resistance. Forsyth's Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey is what the chapter draws on; it is not a treatment of Nyanga or Bantu-area material, which Forsyth does not undertake. The typological framing is what Forsyth supplies for the chapter's comparative engagement, and the contrastive reading depends on having a typology to refuse, which Forsyth's long-arc anchor provides.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic) against which the Mwindo / Kirimu narrative is read as the present book's clearest contrastive-case-tier resistance to the chaoskampf typology; Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Nyanga or Bantu-area material directly, and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Nyanga / Bantu-area content scholarly coverage. The contrastive reading depends on having a typology to refuse, for which Forsyth's long-arc anchor is load-bearing in this chapter as in the preceding non-Eurasian related-tier chapters of the present book.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

The chapter's textual situation is distinctive within the present book in three further methodological respects. It depends on a single mid-twentieth-century recorded performance rather than on a pre-modern manuscript tradition or a deep ethnographic-recording corpus across multiple decades; the recording was made within the colonial-ethnographic context of the late Belgian-colonial period in the eastern Belgian Congo, with the corresponding compositional-context filtering that the colonial-ethnographic situation introduces; and there is no pre-modern attestation of the Mwindo material in any African or external manuscript form against which the 1956 Rureke recording can be cross-checked.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the chapter's textual situation as a single 1956 recorded performance rather than a pre-modern manuscript tradition or deep ethnographic-recording corpus across multiple decades; on the late-Belgian-colonial-period recording context in the eastern Belgian Congo; on the absence of pre-modern attestation of the Mwindo material in any African or external manuscript form against which the 1956 Rureke recording could be cross-checked; per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml special_instructions on acknowledging the oral-performance context (Shé-Kárisi Rureke as the named 1956 narrator).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The shape of that evidence — single 1956 recorded performance, named narrator and locality, late-Belgian-colonial recording context, no pre-modern manuscript attestation — is methodologically load-bearing for the whole chapter, and the claims document marks it explicitly:

[LACUNA: pre-modern (i.e., pre-twentieth-century) attestations of the Mwindo / Kirimu narrative in any written form — African manuscript tradition, Arabic-language chronicle tradition, or earlier European-language traveller's or missionary account — are not available, and the surviving textual witness for the present chapter is the 1956 Bese recording from Shé-Kárisi Rureke published in Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, supplemented by the further Nyanga epic-material recorded by Biebuyck and successors from other named narrators in the 1950s and afterwards (collected in Biebuyck's later publications including *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga*, 1978). Cross-tradition reconstruction is excluded per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" point 1.]

The colonial-ethnographic-mediation caveat applies to the present chapter with particular force. Where the Bida / Wagadu material of chapter 28 of the present book was recorded across multiple early- and mid-twentieth-century European ethnographic projects (Frobenius, Monteil, the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Dieterlen) over a span of decades, the Mwindo material rests on a single 1956 recording made within a single late-Belgian-colonial-period field encounter, with the compositional-context filtering that the colonial-ethnographic situation introduces and with no parallel deep ethnographic-recording corpus to triangulate against. Current scholarship including Biebuyck's own subsequent reflective treatments and Belcher 1999 reads the 1956 Rureke text critically as a starting point rather than as a transparently-faithful record of pre-colonial Nyanga performance.footnote:[Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the late-Belgian-colonial-period recording context within which the 1956 Bese recording from Shé-Kárisi Rureke was made, on the compositional-context filtering that the colonial-ethnographic situation introduces, and on the chapter's textual witness situation as a single field-encounter recording rather than a deep ethnographic-recording corpus; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the critical reading of the 1956 Rureke text as a starting point rather than as a transparently-faithful record of pre-colonial Nyanga performance; per the contrast with the Bida / Wagadu chapter 28 recording-history situation in which a deep multi-recording ethnographic corpus across decades supplies triangulation that the Mwindo single-recording situation does not afford.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

Structurally, the chapter is a contrastive-case-tier (full) treatment with a target of approximately 5,000 narrative words. It presents the Mwindo / Kirimu episode on the Nyanga tradition's own terms — the hero's miraculous birth, his sequence of feats and journeys, the Kirimu episode as one episode within the wider epic's narrative arc, the killing-and-punishment-and-celestial-humiliation sequence, the hero's chastened re-integration into the human world — before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and only then registers the contrastive-case reading per which the narrative's own moral-didactic frame refuses the chaos-order mapping that the chaoskampf typology presupposes. The chapter is the present book's clearest case of a tradition whose framing explicitly rejects the typology, per brief special_instructions and per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" CONTRASTIVE / Inversion entry on Mwindo as the contrastive case alongside Bida (chapter 28) and Nanabozho (chapter 32).footnote:[Per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml on the contrastive-case-tier (full) classification, the 5,000-word target, and the special_instructions requirement that the chapter present the narrative on the tradition's own terms before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, with the special_instructions clause that "the resistance is the data" governing the chapter's treatment; per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" on the Mwindo material's contrastive-case status alongside Bida and Nanabozho.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Because the chapter follows a single recorded performance as its primary text, with related Nyanga epic-material from other named narrators treated as comparative context rather than as parallel attestation, the variant-handling is flagged here in full:

[VARIANT: primary=Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)* — the recorded 1956 Bese performance of Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke (conventionally cited as Shé-Kárisi Rureke), published in 1969 by the University of California Press as the standard English-language critical edition and translation of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode | alt=other named-narrator Nyanga epic-recordings published by Biebuyck and successors from the 1950s fieldwork onward (notably collected in Biebuyck's later *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga*, University of California Press, 1978), which present further Nyanga heroic epic-material from other named tradition-bearers but which the brief explicitly classifies as related comparative material rather than as parallel attestation of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode in the Rureke 1969 anchor text | chosen: A (Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 Rureke text) | reason: per brief 29-mwindo-kirimu.yaml variants.classification single-prevalent and variants.summary explicit specification — "The Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 text (Shé-Kárisi Rureke's performance) is the standard primary text.
