# Input file: chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.adoc

```
[[ch-26-pariacaca-amaru]]
== Pariacaca and the amaru (Huarochirí)

=== Source overview

The story this chapter follows is an early-colonial Andean narrative cycle in which Pariacaca — a storm-wak'a born on the high mountains of Huarochirí as a clutch of falcon-eggs — opposes and defeats the rival wak'a Huallallo Carhuincho and a sequence of further regional rivals across the central-Andean landscape. The serpentine *amaru* runs through these episodes as an ally-monster summoned by defeated rivals, not as a primordial chaos-adversary in the Eurasian sense. The chapter follows the cycle's own framing first, and only afterwards engages the comparativist combat-myth category.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml summary on Pariacaca's opposition to and integration of rival wak'as across the Huarochirí landscape and on the amaru as summoned ally-monster of a defeated rival; Salomon & Urioste 1991, *The Huarochirí Manuscript: A Testament of Ancient and Colonial Andean Religion* (Austin: University of Texas Press, 1991; ISBN 9780292730533), translators' introduction and chs. 6, 8, 21 on the Pariacaca cycle as a sequence of wak'a-combats rather than as a single chaoskampf.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8, 21 (Pariacaca and amaru passages)

The first thing to register about that cycle is the shape of the evidence behind it. The narrative survives in a single early-colonial Quechua-language manuscript conventionally dated to approximately 1608 CE, known in current scholarship as the *Huarochirí Manuscript* and held at the Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid as MS 3169 within a wider compilation of materials connected to the early seventeenth-century Spanish ecclesiastical campaign against indigenous Andean religion in the Lima archdiocese.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the c. 1608 dating of the *Huarochirí Manuscript*, on its Quechua-language compositional medium, and on its preservation as part of MS 3169 at the Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid; on the manuscript's place within the early seventeenth-century materials connected to the anti-idolatry campaign in the Lima archdiocese.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The compilation milieu is Cuzco-born secular priest Francisco de Ávila, who from approximately 1608 CE onward conducted an anti-idolatry investigation in the Huarochirí province of the Lima archdiocese. Ávila used the manuscript's documentation of pre-Christian Andean wak'a-religion as evidence in the prosecution of indigenous religious practitioners and as material for his own anti-idolatry sermons and treatises.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the *Huarochirí Manuscript*'s compilation within the milieu of Francisco de Ávila's anti-idolatry investigation in the Huarochirí province from approximately 1608 CE onward; on the manuscript's documentary purpose in supplying evidence for the prosecution of indigenous religious practitioners and material for Ávila's anti-idolatry sermons and treatises.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The manuscript's authorship is conventionally attributed in current scholarship to a Quechua-speaking indigenous Andean informant or set of informants writing under Ávila's direction. The language of composition is a colonial-Quechua register that preserves substantial pre-Christian religious vocabulary, narrative structure, and place-name attestation, while at the same time being filtered through the documentary conventions of the Spanish-ecclesiastical anti-idolatry investigation.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the indigenous-Andean Quechua-speaking authorship of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* under Ávila's direction; on the colonial-Quechua register of the composition; on the preservation of pre-Christian religious vocabulary, narrative structure, and place-name attestation alongside the Spanish-ecclesiastical filtering.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The *Huarochirí Manuscript* is the sole substantial primary-source attestation for pre-Columbian Huarochirí religion and one of the very few connected Quechua-language narrative-documentary witnesses for any region of the central Andes. The alphabetic-text witness situation for the Andean material is substantially more constrained than the post-conquest textual situation for Mesoamerican religion treated in the preceding chapters of the present book.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the *Huarochirí Manuscript* as the sole substantial primary-source attestation for pre-Columbian Huarochirí religion and as one of the very few connected Quechua-language narrative-documentary witnesses for any region of the central Andes; on the contrast with the more extensive post-conquest textual situation for Mesoamerican religion.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The manuscript itself is organised as a connected sequence of approximately thirty-one chapters and several supplements covering the Huarochirí region's wak'a-cosmography, the Pariacaca cycle, the related cycle of the wak'a Chaupiñamca, the wak'a-genealogies of the constituent ayllus of the region, and the ritual-and-festival apparatus connected to the principal wak'as. The Pariacaca cycle is distributed across several non-contiguous chapters of the manuscript rather than collected as a single connected narrative block.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction and ch. table on the manuscript's organisation as approximately thirty-one chapters with supplements covering wak'a-cosmography, the Pariacaca cycle, the Chaupiñamca cycle, ayllu wak'a-genealogies, and ritual-and-festival apparatus; on the distribution of the Pariacaca cycle across non-contiguous chapters of the manuscript rather than as a single connected narrative block.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 1-31

The principal Pariacaca and amaru passages on which the present chapter draws are concentrated in chapters 6, 8, and 21 of the manuscript: chapter 6 narrates Pariacaca's birth as a clutch of falcon-eggs on the mountain Condorcoto and the birth of his rival Huallallo Carhuincho's children; chapter 8 narrates Pariacaca's combat with Huallallo Carhuincho in the form of a five-day flood-and-storm engagement; chapter 21 supplies further ritual-aetiological material on the Pariacaca cult and on the wak'a-succession pattern that the cycle establishes.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml sources.primary entry on the loc-tag "Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8, 21 (Pariacaca and amaru passages)"; Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 6, 8, 21, on the distribution of the Pariacaca and amaru material across these three chapters with the falcon-eggs birth narrative in ch. 6, the flood-and-storm combat with Huallallo Carhuincho in ch. 8, and the ritual-aetiological material on the Pariacaca cult in ch. 21.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8, 21 (Pariacaca and amaru passages)

The chapter's principal English-language scholarly anchor is Frank Salomon and George L. Urioste's *The Huarochirí Manuscript: A Testament of Ancient and Colonial Andean Religion*, published by the University of Texas Press in 1991. That edition supplies the standard English-language critical edition and translation of the manuscript together with extensive translators' introductory and annotation apparatus on the manuscript's textual history, the colonial-Quechua linguistic features, the wak'a-cosmography, and the area-specialist scholarly readings of the Pariacaca cycle.footnote:[Salomon, F. and Urioste, G.L. (trans. and eds.), *The Huarochirí Manuscript: A Testament of Ancient and Colonial Andean Religion* (Austin: University of Texas Press, 1991; ISBN 9780292730533), as the chapter's principal English-language critical-edition-and-translation anchor with translators' introductory and annotation apparatus on textual history, colonial-Quechua features, wak'a-cosmography, and area-specialist readings; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml sources.primary entry.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

A secondary scholarly anchor sits behind the wider comparative-tradition framing rather than the source-content. Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, supplies the long-arc combat-myth typology — its survey runs from Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic — within which the present chapter situates the Pariacaca cycle as a non-cognate parallel to (rather than a member of) Forsyth's Near-Eastern-and-Judeo-Christian arc. The typological framing — not Andean-material coverage, which Forsyth does not undertake — is what Forsyth supplies for the chapter's comparative engagement with the chaoskampf category.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic) within which the comparativist chaoskampf reading of the Pariacaca cycle would be situated; Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Andean material directly, and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Andean-content scholarly coverage.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

The chapter's textual situation is therefore distinctive within the present book in two further methodological respects. It depends on a single primary-source manuscript with no parallel manuscript witnesses to cross-check its readings; and the manuscript itself is a colonial-period composition whose informants and redactor-context are inseparable from the Spanish-ecclesiastical anti-idolatry investigation under whose auspices the manuscript was produced.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the *Huarochirí Manuscript* as a single-witness source with no parallel manuscript attestations and on the inseparability of the manuscript from the Spanish-ecclesiastical anti-idolatry investigation under whose auspices it was produced; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml summary on the manuscript as the single primary source for the cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The shape of that evidence — single colonial witness, anti-idolatry compositional context, no pre-Columbian alphabetic attestation — is methodologically load-bearing for the whole chapter, and the claims document marks it explicitly:

[LACUNA: pre-conquest Quechua-language alphabetic-text attestations of the Pariacaca cycle are not available, since pre-Columbian Andean record-keeping practices used the *khipu* (knotted-cord) medium rather than alphabetic writing for narrative material, and the surviving textual witness to the cycle is therefore the single early-colonial *Huarochirí Manuscript* (c. 1608) compiled within the milieu of Francisco de Ávila's anti-idolatry investigation. Pre-conquest performance contexts (Pariacaca-cult ritual at the named mountain shrines of the Huarochirí region; ayllu-level wak'a-veneration; *capacocha* and pilgrimage practice) are partially recoverable through the manuscript itself, through Spanish-administrative documentation of the anti-idolatry campaigns, and through the archaeological record of the central Andean ritual-architectural sites. | source: brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml on the Huarochirí Manuscript as single primary source; Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the textual situation of pre-conquest Andean religion and on the *khipu*-rather-than-alphabetic record-keeping of pre-Columbian Andean polities. | scholarly_reconstruction: yes — the chapter's narrative reconstruction proceeds entirely within the *Huarochirí Manuscript* itself, treating chs. 6, 8, and 21 as parts of a single Pariacaca cycle per the brief's composite-editorial classification, with Salomon & Urioste 1991 as the principal interpretive synthesis. Cross-tradition reconstruction is excluded per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" point 1.]

The post-conquest filtering caveat the present chapter must register applies with particular force to the Pariacaca material. Where the post-conquest Mexica textual record (chapter 24 of the present book) involves Spanish-friar redaction of Mexica narrative material recorded by Nahua informants for documentary purposes, the *Huarochirí Manuscript* was compiled by an explicitly anti-idolatry investigator (Ávila) precisely so that the religion the manuscript documents could be the object of ecclesiastical prosecution and suppression — a more polemical compositional context than the Sahagún or Durán situations, requiring corresponding methodological caution.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the *Huarochirí Manuscript*'s composition for the explicit purpose of supplying evidence and material for Ávila's anti-idolatry investigation and prosecution; on the methodological situation that this polemical compositional context creates for current scholarly use of the manuscript; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

Structurally, the chapter is a related-tier (full) treatment with a target of approximately 4,000 narrative words. It presents the Pariacaca cycle as a connected sequence of wak'a-combats rather than as a single cosmogonic combat, and embeds the area-specialist counter-framing — the wak'a-succession reading per Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, and Dean — alongside the narrative skeleton rather than as a separately-located commentary. Per brief special_instructions, the area-specialist framing is load-bearing and is presented before any comparative chaoskampf invocation.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml on the related-tier (full) classification, the 4,000-word target, the special_instructions requirement that the chapter open with the Huarochirí Manuscript's own framing (Quechua-language early-colonial text, recorded by an Andean community) before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and the area-specialist citation policy naming Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, and Dean.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

What follows works through the cycle in order. The next section sketches the Huarochirí region and the wak'a-cosmography of pre-Columbian central Andean religion as the immediate context for the narrative; the chapter then takes up Pariacaca's birth as the five falcon-eggs on Mount Condorcoto, the five-day combat with Huallallo Carhuincho, the role of the amaru as ally-monster of defeated rivals, the cult and the ritual aftermath at chapter 21 of the manuscript, and the chapter's typological place and area-specialist framing within the wider book.

=== The Huarochirí region and the wak'a-cosmography

The narrative's geographical setting is the Huarochirí province on the western slopes of the central Andes east of present-day Lima, Peru — a high-altitude landscape of glaciated mountain peaks, intermontane valleys, and the upper drainages of the rivers that descend from the western Cordillera to the Pacific coast. The cycle is rooted in specific named landscape features within this geography that function in the narrative as the bodies and the sites of the named wak'as.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction and chs. 6-21 on the Huarochirí provincial geography of the central-Andean western Cordillera east of present-day Lima, on the high-altitude landscape of glaciated peaks and intermontane valleys, and on the rooting of the Pariacaca cycle in specific named landscape features that function as the bodies and the sites of the named wak'as.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction and chs. 6-21

The cycle's principal cosmographic framework is the wak'a-cosmography of pre-Columbian central Andean religion. The landscape is conceived as populated by *wak'as* — sacred power-beings (Quechua; older Spanish-derived spelling: huaca) — that are simultaneously persons with biographies, places (mountains, springs, stones, lakes), and ancestral sources of the human ayllus that descend from them. The *Huarochirí Manuscript* records a landscape populated by *wak'as* in hierarchical and combative relationships rather than in a chaos-versus-order opposition.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the wak'a-cosmography of pre-Columbian central Andean religion, on the wak'a as simultaneously person, place, and ancestral source, and on the *Huarochirí Manuscript*'s recording of a landscape of wak'as in hierarchical and combative relationships; per glossary 'wak'a' first_mention_gloss on the pattern as wak'a-succession rather than chaos-versus-order.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

Pariacaca himself is, in the manuscript, simultaneously a person (a divine figure born and acting in narrative time), a mountain (the eponymous Pariacaca peak in the Yauyos region of the western Cordillera, a high glaciated massif among the headwaters of multiple drainages of the central Andean cordillera, including the Mantaro and Cañete river systems), and an ancestral wak'a of the Yauyos people of the Huarochirí region, whose ayllus trace their descent from him and from his sons. This triple identity person-place-ancestor is the cycle's load-bearing cosmographic premise rather than an interpretive overlay.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 6 and 8 on Pariacaca's triple identity as person, mountain, and ancestral wak'a of the Yauyos people of the Huarochirí region; on the eponymous Pariacaca peak as a high glaciated massif among the headwaters of the central Andean cordillera with the Mantaro and Cañete river systems among the principal drainages of the wider Yauyos / Huarochirí area (the precise watershed-boundary formulation against Salomon & Urioste 1991's translators' introduction and against a modern Andean hydrographic reference is to be confirmed at chapter-draft); on the ayllu-descent of the Yauyos from Pariacaca and his sons; per glossary 'Pariacaca' first_mention_gloss on Pariacaca as Andean storm-wak'a of the Huarochirí region.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8

The cycle's principal antagonist is the rival wak'a Huallallo Carhuincho — an earlier-attested wak'a of the same region whose hegemony the Pariacaca narrative replaces. In the manuscript Huallallo is associated with a fire-and-volcanic-heat thematic register, with child-eating, and with the eastern (Antisuyu, Amazonian-slope) direction of the central-Andean cardinal-quartering. These features distinguish him narratively from Pariacaca's storm-and-rain-and-cold thematic register and the western direction of his association.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 6, 8 on Huallallo Carhuincho as the rival wak'a whose hegemony Pariacaca replaces, on the fire-and-volcanic-heat thematic register associated with Huallallo, on the child-eating motif, and on the eastern (Antisuyu, Amazonian-slope) direction of his association; on the contrast with Pariacaca's storm-and-rain-and-cold register and the western directional association.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8

=== Pariacaca's birth: the five falcon-eggs on Mount Condorcoto

The cycle begins with Pariacaca's birth on the mountain Condorcoto in the form of a clutch of five eggs that hatch into five falcons. The manuscript presents this avian-multiple birth as the narrative's distinctive opening rather than as a mammalian or anthropomorphic generation, and the five-fold birth supplies the numerological structure for the cycle's subsequent narrative pattern: Pariacaca's five sons in some renderings, his five-day combat with Huallallo Carhuincho, the five regional ayllus that descend from him.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 6 on Pariacaca's birth on Mount Condorcoto in the form of a clutch of five eggs that hatch into five falcons, on the avian-multiple-birth pattern as the cycle's distinctive opening, and on the five-fold structure that this birth-narrative supplies for the subsequent narrative pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 6

The five falcons of Pariacaca's birth subsequently transform into five anthropomorphic divine figures. Some passages of the manuscript refer to these figures as the five Pariacacas or as Pariacaca and his four brothers; other passages render them as Pariacaca and his sons. The five-fold person-multiplicity persists through the cycle as a load-bearing feature of Pariacaca's narrative identity rather than resolving to a single individual figure as the cycle progresses.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 6 and following on the transformation of the five falcons into five anthropomorphic figures, on the manuscript's varying renderings of these figures as the five Pariacacas / Pariacaca and four brothers / Pariacaca and his sons, and on the persistence of the five-fold person-multiplicity through the cycle as a load-bearing feature rather than a resolved-to-single-individual pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 6

The narrative simultaneously narrates the birth of Pariacaca's narrative foil Huallallo Carhuincho's children in chapter 6, with the parallel-births framing setting up the immediately-following combat between the two wak'a-lineages. The manuscript places the two birth-events in narrative parallel rather than treating Pariacaca's birth as cosmogonically prior to Huallallo's existence: Huallallo Carhuincho is presented as already established in the region as the dominant wak'a at the moment of Pariacaca's hatching.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 6 on the parallel narration of Pariacaca's birth and the birth of Huallallo Carhuincho's children, on the parallel-births framing as the narrative-structural setup for the subsequent combat between the two wak'a-lineages, and on Huallallo Carhuincho's already-established hegemonic position in the region at the moment of Pariacaca's hatching.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 6

The narrative's pre-existing wak'a-political situation is significant for the cycle's classification within the present book's typology. Pariacaca acts within an already-existing wak'a-cosmos in which Huallallo Carhuincho holds the dominant position, and the crisis the cycle addresses is the displacement of one hegemonic wak'a by another rather than the establishment of cosmic order ex novo — a pattern that places the Pariacaca cycle within wak'a-succession rather than within cosmogonic-combat in the strong scope.md sense.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 6, 8 on the pre-existing wak'a-political situation in the Huarochirí region at the moment of Pariacaca's birth, on Huallallo Carhuincho's already-hegemonic position, and on the cycle's narrative work as the displacement of one hegemonic wak'a by another rather than the establishment of cosmic order ex novo; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml summary on the wak'a-succession framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8

=== The combat with Huallallo Carhuincho: storm-and-flood

Pariacaca's principal combat in the cycle, narrated in chapter 8 of the manuscript, is his five-day engagement with Huallallo Carhuincho. The two wak'as confront one another on the high mountain landscape, with Pariacaca deploying torrential rain, hail, and lightning from the western side and Huallallo Carhuincho deploying fire and volcanic heat from the eastern side. The natural-meteorological register of the combat — water-and-cold against fire-and-heat — is the narrative's principal combat-modality.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 8 on Pariacaca's five-day combat with Huallallo Carhuincho, on the high-mountain-landscape setting of the engagement, on Pariacaca's torrential rain / hail / lightning weapons from the western side, on Huallallo's fire-and-volcanic-heat weapons from the eastern side, and on the water-and-cold-versus-fire-and-heat register as the principal combat-modality.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 8

In the chapter 8 combat-narrative, Pariacaca deploys his storm-power as the five Pariacacas acting jointly. Each of the five is presented as releasing rain or hail or lightning from a separate direction, and the combined storm-attack is the cycle's load-bearing image of the five-fold-Pariacaca's combat-effectiveness. The five-fold image of the storm is a distinctive feature of the Andean material that has no exact parallel in the storm-deity narratives of the Eurasian traditions in the present book's other chapters.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 8 on the five Pariacacas acting jointly in the combat with Huallallo Carhuincho, on each of the five releasing rain / hail / lightning from a separate direction, on the combined storm-attack as the cycle's load-bearing combat-image, and on the five-fold-storm pattern as a distinctive feature of the Andean material; per glossary 'Pariacaca' first_mention_gloss on the storm-wak'a character.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 8

The combat's outcome is the defeat of Huallallo Carhuincho and his expulsion from the Huarochirí region eastward into the Antisuyu (Amazonian-slope) zone. Huallallo Carhuincho is not killed, but rather displaced — a feature of the narrative that distinguishes the Pariacaca cycle from the kill-the-adversary pattern of the Eurasian chaoskampf material and that aligns the cycle with the wak'a-succession reading of Salomon, Urioste, and the wider Andean specialist tradition.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 8 on the combat's outcome as Huallallo Carhuincho's defeat and eastward expulsion into the Antisuyu zone rather than as Huallallo's killing, on the displacement-rather-than-killing pattern as a feature of the Andean cycle that distinguishes it from the kill-the-adversary pattern of Eurasian chaoskampf, and on the alignment of this feature with the wak'a-succession reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 8

The post-combat geographical settlement of the cycle's outcome is the establishment of Pariacaca's hegemony over the Huarochirí region's wak'a-cosmography. Pariacaca takes possession of the high mountain peak that bears his name, his five sons (or brothers) take possession of subsidiary peaks and ritual centres in the region, and Huallallo Carhuincho's former sphere is reorganised under Pariacaca's authority. The manuscript presents this settlement as a wak'a-political reorganisation of the existing landscape rather than as a cosmogonic establishment of the landscape itself.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 8 on the post-combat geographical settlement of the cycle's outcome, on Pariacaca's possession of the eponymous mountain peak, on his five sons or brothers taking subsidiary peaks and ritual centres, and on the wak'a-political reorganisation of the existing landscape as the settlement-pattern; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the political-theological function of wak'a-succession.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 8

=== The amaru: ally-monster of the defeated rival

The serpentine *amaru* figures across the Pariacaca cycle as an ally-monster summoned by a defeated rival wak'a in the course of further engagements. Its narrative role is the discharge of the defeated wak'a's residual combat-capacity rather than the embodiment of a primordial chaos-adversary in the Eurasian chaoskampf sense, and the amaru's defeat by Pariacaca (or by his sons) is typically a follow-on to the principal wak'a-combat rather than the cycle's central engagement.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 8 and following on the amaru as ally-monster summoned by a defeated rival wak'a, on the amaru's narrative role as the discharge of the defeated wak'a's residual combat-capacity, and on the amaru's defeat as a follow-on to the principal wak'a-combat; per glossary 'amaru' first_mention_gloss on the amaru as Andean serpent-figure summoned as ally-monster by a defeated rival wak'a rather than as primordial chaos.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

The amaru is described in the manuscript as a giant serpent-figure of the lake-and-river waters of the high Andean landscape, often associated with specific lakes and springs in the Huarochirí region and with the discharge of those bodies of water as the amaru moves. In the manuscript's narrative register the amaru is therefore a figure of the lake-being / water-serpent type rather than of the primordial-cosmic-serpent type that is load-bearing in the Eurasian chaoskampf material.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, on the amaru's description as a giant serpent-figure of the lake-and-river waters of the high Andean landscape, on the amaru's specific associations with lakes and springs in the Huarochirí region, on the discharge of those bodies of water as the amaru moves, and on the lake-being / water-serpent typological character of the figure rather than the primordial-cosmic-serpent character of Eurasian chaoskampf material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

The amaru's defeat in the cycle is typically narrated as a cutting, splitting, or driving-into-a-confined-space rather than as a comprehensive destruction. The defeated amaru's body becomes a feature of the landscape — a lake's outline, a watercourse's bend, a stone formation — rather than being reduced to nothing. Like the displacement-rather-than-killing of Huallallo Carhuincho, this pattern distinguishes the Andean material from the kill-and-eradicate pattern of much Eurasian chaoskampf.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, on the amaru's defeat as cutting, splitting, or driving-into-a-confined-space rather than comprehensive destruction, on the defeated amaru's body becoming a feature of the landscape (lake outline, watercourse bend, stone formation), and on the parallel between this displacement-rather-than-killing pattern and the wider cycle's wak'a-succession-rather-than-eradication framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

Because the chapter treats chapters 6, 8, and 21 of the manuscript as parts of a single composite-editorial cycle rather than as a single combat or as three discrete narrative units, the variant-handling is flagged here in full:

[VARIANT: primary=composite-editorial reading per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml variants.classification, treating chs. 6, 8, and 21 of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* as parts of a single Pariacaca cycle in which the falcon-eggs birth (ch. 6), the five-day combat with Huallallo Carhuincho (ch. 8), and the ritual-aetiological material (ch. 21) together constitute the chapter's narrative skeleton, with the amaru appearing across the cycle as a recurring ally-monster figure summoned by defeated rivals rather than as a single named adversary | alt=alternative organising principles are available for the manuscript's Pariacaca material — for example, treating chs. 6 and 8 together as the Huallallo-Carhuincho combat-narrative and treating ch. 21 separately as a discrete ritual-aetiological appendix; or organising the material around the five Pariacacas individually rather than around the cycle as a whole | chosen: composite-editorial-cycle-skeleton | reason: per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml variants.classification: composite-editorial; the chapter treats the chs. 6, 8, 21 material as a cycle rather than as a single combat per the brief's explicit framing, and Salomon & Urioste 1991's translators' introduction supports the composite-editorial reading as the most useful organisational principle for an English-language overview chapter.]

The wider sequence of Pariacaca's engagements across the manuscript includes further combats and integrations of subsidiary rival wak'as beyond the principal Huallallo Carhuincho engagement. The manuscript narrates Pariacaca's encounters with the wak'as of further regions of Huarochirí and his sons' encounters with their counterparts. The cycle's overall pattern is the gradual integration of the regional wak'a-landscape under Pariacaca-cult authority across multiple narrative episodes rather than a single decisive combat-and-resolution.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, chs. 8-21 on the wider sequence of Pariacaca's engagements with subsidiary rival wak'as beyond the principal Huallallo Carhuincho combat, on the encounters with the wak'as of further regions of Huarochirí, on the engagements of Pariacaca's sons with their counterparts, and on the cycle's overall pattern as gradual integration of the regional wak'a-landscape under Pariacaca-cult authority across multiple episodes rather than a single decisive combat-and-resolution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8-21

=== The cult of Pariacaca and the ritual aftermath

Chapter 21 of the manuscript supplies the principal ritual-aetiological material on the Pariacaca cult. The manuscript records the cult's pilgrimage practice (with named pilgrim-routes converging on the Pariacaca peak), the calendrical festivals associated with the wak'a, and the ritual-and-sacrificial apparatus through which the regional ayllus maintained their relationships with Pariacaca and his sons under both pre-Columbian and early-colonial conditions.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 21 on the principal ritual-aetiological material on the Pariacaca cult, on the cult's pilgrimage practice with named pilgrim-routes converging on the Pariacaca peak, on the calendrical festivals associated with the wak'a, and on the ritual-and-sacrificial apparatus through which the regional ayllus maintained their relationships with Pariacaca and his sons.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 21

The ritual aetiologies that chapter 21 supplies tie specific elements of the combat-narrative to specific contemporary (early-colonial) ritual practices: the five-day-combat structure to the calendrical structure of certain festivals; the falcon-eggs birth to specific ritual objects and offerings; particular landscape features named in the combat-narrative to particular pilgrimage destinations. The aetiological linking is a distinctive feature of the Andean material that integrates the narrative cycle into the regional ritual life across the moment of textual composition.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, ch. 21 on the ritual aetiologies that link specific elements of the Pariacaca combat-narrative to specific early-colonial ritual practices, on the five-day-combat structure linked to calendrical festival-structure, on the falcon-eggs birth linked to ritual objects and offerings, on landscape features linked to pilgrimage destinations, and on the aetiological-linking pattern as a distinctive feature integrating narrative and ritual.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 21

The Pariacaca cult's reach within and beyond the Huarochirí region is significant for the cycle's status. Under the late-Inca-period (fifteenth century CE) imperial reorganisation of central-Andean wak'a-religion the Pariacaca cult was integrated into the imperial pilgrimage-network with the Pariacaca peak being a recognised pilgrimage destination at imperial-administrative level, and under early-colonial conditions the cult continued in compromised forms despite Ávila's anti-idolatry investigations.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction and ch. 21 on the Pariacaca cult's reach within and beyond the Huarochirí region, on the late-Inca-period imperial reorganisation under which the cult was integrated into the imperial pilgrimage-network and the Pariacaca peak was a recognised pilgrimage destination at imperial-administrative level, and on the cult's continuation in compromised forms under early-colonial conditions despite Ávila's anti-idolatry investigations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction and ch. 21

The chapter's reading of the cycle's principal narrative work as wak'a-succession rather than as cosmogonic combat is itself an editorial synthesis drawn from the brief, from Salomon & Urioste 1991's translators' introduction, and from the wider Andean-specialist tradition; it is flagged as such:

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Pariacaca cycle's principal narrative work as wak'a-succession (the displacement of Huallallo Carhuincho's hegemony by Pariacaca's, with the regional landscape reorganised under Pariacaca-cult authority but pre-existing the cycle in its physical form) rather than as cosmogonic combat (the establishment of the cosmos itself from or after the combat) is a synthesis of the brief's framing per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml summary, of Salomon & Urioste 1991's translators' introductory and annotation apparatus, and of the brief's named area-specialist tradition (Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, Dean) — treated together as the chapter's load-bearing reading. | basis: brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml explicitly classifies the cycle's pattern as wak'a-succession and explicitly contrasts it with cosmos-versus-chaos combat; Salomon & Urioste 1991's translators' introduction develops the wak'a-succession reading at length; the Andean specialist tradition cited in the brief's special_instructions (Taylor, Dean, with Salomon and Urioste already in the chapter's anchor) converges on the wak'a-succession reading. | risk: alternative comparativist readings have been advanced (older twentieth-century structuralist readings tended to assimilate the Pariacaca-Huallallo combat to a cosmos-versus-chaos pattern); the chapter's wak'a-succession reading is the area-specialist consensus reading per the brief but is not the only available scholarly position, and the chapter holds it as the load-bearing reading on the strength of the area-specialist citation policy at scope.md §"Appropriation protocol".]

=== Typology, area-specialist framing, and forward-links

The Pariacaca cycle's place within the present book's chaoskampf typology is — when taken in the area-specialist reading per the brief's framing — a related-tier (rather than core-tier) case in which the combat-pattern is structurally present (storm-wak'a versus fire-and-volcanic-heat-wak'a; storm-wak'a's sons versus serpentine ally-monsters of further rivals) but the cosmogonic and order-versus-chaos load-bearing functions of Eurasian chaoskampf are not load-bearing for the Andean material, with the cycle's principal work being wak'a-succession-and-cult-establishment rather than cosmos-establishment.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml summary on the wak'a-succession framing of the cycle and on the contrast with cosmos-versus-chaos combat; scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the cosmogonic-combat work is weaker; Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the wak'a-succession reading as the area-specialist consensus reading of the cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

That place is the specific outcome of an area-specialist reading that resists the chaos-versus-order framing of the Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf category, and the chapter flags the resulting appropriation issue explicitly under the marker the project reserves for cases of this kind:

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: Andean-studies area specialists (Frank Salomon and George L. Urioste in their 1991 critical edition; with the further area-specialists Gérald Taylor in his Lima IFEA critical edition of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* and Carolyn Dean on Andean ritual-political imagination, named in the brief's special_instructions) read the Pariacaca cycle as wak'a-succession (the displacement of one hegemonic regional wak'a by another within an already-existing wak'a-cosmography) rather than as chaoskampf in the strong cosmos-versus-chaos sense; the area-specialist objection is to the chaos-order framing rather than to combat-myth framing as such, since the cycle does narrate combat between named divine figures and a wak'a-political reorganisation does follow from the combat-outcome — the area-specialist reading is therefore a milder objection than (e.g.) the Naumann objection to the chaoskampf reading of the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi material in chapter 23 (where the area-specialists object to the combat-myth framing itself in favour of a sword-etiology and metallurgical-allegory reading) or the Hallowell-Johnston-Smith-Vizenor objection in chapter 32 (where Anishinaabe-studies area specialists explicitly reject combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing). The Pariacaca cycle accommodates some combat-myth framing — the combat with Huallallo Carhuincho and the engagements with the amaru-summoning rivals are structurally combats — but does not accommodate the chaos-versus-order framing that Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf-typology presupposes. Part II C7 (inversion / transformation; ch. 40 of the present volume) and Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation; ch. 41) are the loci where the comparative analysis must address this objection rather than paper over it; the present chapter functions as a worked instance of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement at scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3, and forwards the objection to C7 and C8 for the comparative-engagement work that scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 2 requires of the comparative chapters. The brief's special_instructions explicitly characterise the case as "milder than Nanabozho/Mishipeshu" because the area-specialist reading does accommodate some combat-myth framing — just not the chaos-order framing — so the present chapter's task is to register the objection to the chaos-order framing while preserving the cycle's connected-narrative skeleton for comparative engagement at C7 and C8.]

The chapter's structural parallel to the integration-after-combat pattern of the Marduk cycle in Mesopotamia (chapter 3 of the present volume) is registered by the brief in its cross_cultural_parallels entry. Where Marduk's victory over Tiamat is followed by the recitation of his fifty names by the assembled gods in the closing tablets of the *Enūma Eliš* — a passage in which Marduk's victory consolidates rival power-bases under his own authority rather than eradicating them — Pariacaca's victory over Huallallo Carhuincho is similarly followed by the integration of the defeated rival's former sphere under Pariacaca's authority via the wak'a-succession pattern. The parallel is structural and not transmissional, and is registered for Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) rather than Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony).footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml cross_cultural_parallels entry on the structural parallel between Pariacaca's integration of defeated wak'as and Marduk's incorporation of the fifty names at the end of *Enūma Eliš*, on victory-as-consolidation-of-rival-power-bases rather than victory-as-eradication; on the parallel as structural rather than transmissional; on the registration for Part II C2 rather than Part II C1.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8, 21

The cycle's distinctive contribution to the present book's wider comparative argument is the amaru-as-ally-of-rival pattern. Where Eurasian chaoskampf-tradition serpents (Tiamat, Vṛtra, Apep, Lôtan, Aži Dahāka, Jörmungandr) are typically presented as primordial cosmic adversaries whose defeat is the cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing act, the amaru in the *Huarochirí Manuscript* is presented as a summoned agent of a defeated rival wak'a — a serpentine ally rather than a primordial chaos — and the structural difference is itself one of the chapter's load-bearing contributions to the methods sidebar (chapter 33) and to Part II C7-C8.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml cross_cultural_parallels entry on the amaru-as-ally-of-rival pattern as distinctive to the Andean material; per glossary 'amaru' first_mention_gloss on the amaru as ally-monster of a defeated rival rather than primordial chaos; Salomon & Urioste 1991 on the structural difference between the Andean amaru and Eurasian primordial-cosmic-serpent figures.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C8 (the comparative chapter on the refusal and the appropriation problem, ch. 41 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes a working example of the *milder appropriation* sub-problem: a story in which the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaos-order framing (but not to combat-myth framing as such), with the chapter's task being to hold the comparative chaoskampf category loosely as scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" requires, and to register the wak'a-succession reading as the area-specialist consensus while preserving the cycle's combat-and-integration narrative for comparative engagement.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem), on the chapter's status as a milder case than Nanabozho/Mishipeshu, and on the area-specialist reading accommodating some combat-myth framing but not the chaos-order framing; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 2 on the comparative chapter's task of addressing area-specialist objections rather than papering over them.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to Part II C7 (the comparative chapter on the inversion / transformation problem, ch. 40 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes a working example of the *transformation* sub-problem: a story in which the chaoskampf category is transformed when the area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing, in this case from cosmos-versus-chaos combat to wak'a-succession. This contribution sits alongside the chapter 23 Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi contribution to C7 in the present book's pattern of related-tier non-Eurasian chapters that reframe the chaoskampf category from outside.footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem); per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C7 on the inversion-and-transformation comparative chapter; per the chapter's structural parallel to the chapter 23 Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi contribution to C7 as a related-tier non-Eurasian chapter reframing the chaoskampf category from outside.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The chapter's contribution to the methods sidebar (chapter 33 of the present volume, between Part I and Part II) is on three methodological points: the area-specialist citation policy (Salomon & Urioste 1991 cited as the principal anchor with Taylor and Dean named in body prose per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol"), the post-conquest filtering issue (the *Huarochirí Manuscript* being a colonial-period document compiled within the milieu of Ávila's anti-idolatry investigation, which the chapter handles via the [LACUNA] marker and via continuous methodological registration), and the wak'a-succession-versus-chaoskampf framing (which the chapter handles via the [APPROPRIATION-FLAG:] marker and which is forwarded to C7 and C8 for comparative engagement).footnote:[Per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy and on the post-conquest-filtering methodological situation; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement, the post-conquest source-record-filtering issue, and the area-specialist-objection registration requirement; chapter 33 methods sidebar (per toc.yaml) as the chapter that articulates these methodological points at full length.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to chapter 24 on Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, with which the present chapter shares the Americas geographic cluster (cluster 10 per scope.md §"Book structure") and the post-conquest textual-record situation but contrasts in tier (chapter 24 is core-tier with full architectural-iconographic embedding at the Mexica state's political-ritual centre; the present chapter is related-tier with a single colonial-Quechua manuscript witness compiled under explicit anti-idolatry auspices) and in framing-stance (chapter 24 reads the Coatepec narrative as cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat per the area-specialist consensus; the present chapter reads the Pariacaca cycle as wak'a-succession-not-chaoskampf per the area-specialist consensus).footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 10 (Americas) on the chapter ordering of 24 (Huitzilopochtli core-tier), 25 (Mesoamerican family related-tier), 26 (Pariacaca related-tier), 27 (Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren related-family); per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml on the core-tier classification of Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec on the basis of the area-specialist combat-cosmogony consensus; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml on the related-tier classification of the Pariacaca cycle on the basis of the area-specialist wak'a-succession-not-chaoskampf reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The Pariacaca cycle's cultural-historical importance within the central-Andean tradition and within current Andean-studies scholarship is significant. The *Huarochirí Manuscript* is one of the most-cited primary sources in current scholarship on pre-Columbian Andean religion, the Pariacaca cycle is one of the manuscript's principal narrative sequences, and the cycle has been extensively treated in the scholarly literature on wak'a-religion, on colonial-period extirpation campaigns, and on the cultural-historical significance of the Andean wak'a-cosmography for current scholarly understanding of the pre-Columbian central Andes.footnote:[Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the *Huarochirí Manuscript* as one of the most-cited primary sources in current scholarship on pre-Columbian Andean religion, on the Pariacaca cycle as one of the manuscript's principal narrative sequences, and on the cycle's extensive treatment in the scholarly literature on wak'a-religion, on colonial-period extirpation campaigns, and on the wak'a-cosmography of the pre-Columbian central Andes.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesoamerica (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — preceding Americas-cluster Mexica core-tier chapter; structural and framing-stance contrast between the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat reading of the Coatepec narrative (area-specialist consensus per Matos Moctezuma 1988, Carrasco 1999, López Austin 1993) and the wak'a-succession reading of the Pariacaca cycle (area-specialist consensus per Salomon & Urioste 1991, Taylor, Dean); two distinct Americas-cluster framing-stances within a single geographic cluster, exhibiting the present book's willingness to follow area-specialist consensus rather than imposing a uniform chaoskampf framing across non-Eurasian traditions; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — structural parallel between Pariacaca's integration of defeated wak'as into the Pariacaca-cult sphere and Marduk's incorporation of the fifty names by the assembled gods at the end of *Enūma Eliš*; victory consolidates rival power-bases rather than eradicating them; the parallel is structural rather than transmissional, and is registered for Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) rather than Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) since the Pariacaca cycle is wak'a-succession rather than cosmos-establishment; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml cross_cultural_parallels.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: China (Gonggong, Ch. 21) — water-and-mountain-and-cosmographic-reorganisation parallel: where Gonggong's striking of Mount Buzhou tilts the cosmos and reorganises the hydrography of China, Pariacaca's storm-combat with Huallallo Carhuincho reorganises the wak'a-political landscape of Huarochirí; both narratives use water-and-mountain as the principal combat-elements and result in a landscape-cosmographic reorganisation, although the Gonggong narrative is cosmogonic in the strong sense (the present features of the cosmos emerge from the combat) while the Pariacaca narrative is wak'a-political (the existing landscape is reorganised under new wak'a-cult authority).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Anatolia (Illuyanka, Ch. 6) — serpent-lake-flood comparative pattern: the amaru's lake-and-spring-water associations and discharge-of-water-when-moving features pair structurally with the Hittite Illuyanka material's serpent-and-storm-combat pattern, although the Pariacaca cycle's amaru is an ally-monster summoned by a defeated rival rather than a single principal adversary; the comparison is for Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) and for the methods sidebar (Ch. 33) on the boundary between primordial-cosmic-serpent and lake-being / water-serpent typological figures.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi (Ch. 23) — co-related-tier non-Eurasian chapter where the area-specialist tradition objects to the chaoskampf framing on grounds of an alternative load-bearing function (sword-etiology and metallurgical-allegory in the Susanoo case; wak'a-succession in the Pariacaca case); the present chapter and Ch. 23 are paired as worked instances of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement, with both chapters opening with the tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category; both chapters contribute to Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem) and C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem) as related-tier non-Eurasian reframings.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Nanabozho / Mishipeshu (Ch. 32) — the present chapter is a milder case than the Anishinaabe refusal: where the Hallowell-Johnston-Smith-Vizenor area-specialist tradition for the Anishinaabe material objects to the combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing itself in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing, the Salomon-Urioste-Taylor-Dean area-specialist tradition for the Pariacaca material accommodates combat-myth framing while objecting to the chaos-order framing; the contrast between the two cases is itself one of the methods sidebar's (Ch. 33) load-bearing illustrations of the spectrum of area-specialist objection-strengths within the present book; per brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions on the milder-than-Nanabozho characterisation.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the present chapter is a worked instance of the appropriation-protocol's outside-framing requirement (scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3): the chapter exhibits in concrete narrative-and-citation-discipline detail what it looks like to open with the tradition's own framing (Quechua-language early-colonial text, recorded under Spanish-friar anti-idolatry auspices, with the wak'a-cosmography as the principal interpretive frame per Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, Dean) before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category, and to hold the comparative category loosely where area-specialists require it; the chapter further exhibits the post-conquest source-record-filtering issue at its sharpest (Ávila's anti-idolatry investigation as the more polemical compositional context than the Sahagún or Durán situations of Ch. 24).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) — the Pariacaca cycle does sovereignty-establishment work for the Pariacaca cult and for the Yauyos ayllus that descend from Pariacaca, with the cycle's wak'a-succession pattern parallel to the sovereignty-establishment combats of Baal/Yamm, Zeus/Typhon, Indra/Vṛtra, Thraētaona, Kumarbi/Ullikummi, and Ninurta/Anzû, but with the area-specialist framing as wak'a-succession rather than as sovereignty-versus-chaos combat; the present chapter is a non-Eurasian comparandum for C2 in the wak'a-succession register.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C7 (the inversion / transformation problem, Ch. 40) — the present chapter is a working example of the *transformation* sub-problem: a story in which the chaoskampf category is transformed when the area-specialist account (wak'a-succession rather than cosmos-versus-chaos combat) is taken as load-bearing; this is to be distinguished from the inversion sub-problem proper, anchored by the Bida material in chapter 28 (where the slaying inverts the order-establishing function), and contributes to C7 alongside the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi contribution from chapter 23.
```
